```
│             │            │  └─ filter, score,  │                │ ASCII table │
│             │            │     sort (MergeSort)│                │             │
│             │            │                     │                │             │
│             │            │ MergeSorter         │                │             │
│             │            │  └─ sort()          │                │             │
└─────────────┘            └─────────────────────┘                └─────────────┘
```

> "The **Model** defines data. The **Controller** processes it. The **View** presents it. None of these layers know the internal details of the others — that's **separation of concerns**."

---

### Slide 4 — Object-Oriented Programming Deep Dive (3 minutes)

**What to say:**

> "Our code demonstrates all four OOP principles. Let me show each one concretely."

#### 1. Abstraction — `AudioEntity.java`

> "This is an **interface**. It defines a contract: any audio entity must have `getPopularity()`, `getDanceability()`, `getCompositeScore()`, and `toAsciiRow()`. The interface says **what** a track can do — it says nothing about **how**."

```java
public interface AudioEntity {
    String getTrackId();
    int getPopularity();
    double getDanceability();
    double getCompositeScore();
    String toAsciiRow(int rank);
}
```

#### 2. Encapsulation — `BaseEntity.java`

> "All 13 fields in `BaseEntity` are **private**. No external class can modify `popularity` or `danceability` directly. They must go through getters and setters. This is **data hiding** — the internal state is protected."

```java
public abstract class BaseEntity implements AudioEntity {
    private String trackId;        // ← private
    private int popularity;         // ← private
    private double danceability;    // ← private
    // ... 10 more private fields

    public int getPopularity() { return popularity; }  // ← controlled access
    public void setPopularity(int p) { this.popularity = p; }  // ← controlled mutation
}
```

#### 3. Inheritance — `TrackRecord extends BaseEntity`

> "`TrackRecord` doesn't re-declare `trackId`, `popularity`, or `danceability`. It **inherits** them from `BaseEntity` through `super(...)`. It also inherits `getCompositeScore()`. It only adds what's unique to it — the `toAsciiRow()` formatting."

```java
public class TrackRecord extends BaseEntity {
    public TrackRecord(...) {
        super(trackId, trackName, artists, ...);  // ← calls parent constructor
    }
}
```

#### 4. Polymorphism — `toAsciiRow(int rank)`

> "This is the polymorphic method. It's declared as **abstract** in `BaseEntity` and **overridden** in `TrackRecord`. When the Controller calls `entity.toAsciiRow(rank)`, it doesn't know the concrete type — it just knows it's an `AudioEntity`. At runtime, Java resolves it to `TrackRecord`'s implementation. If we later added `PodcastRecord extends BaseEntity`, the same Controller code would work without changes."

```java
// In BaseController:
public String formatTopN(List<? extends AudioEntity> results, int n) {
    for (int i = 0; i < n; i++) {
        sb.append(results.get(i).toAsciiRow(i + 1));  // ← polymorphic call
    }
}
```

---

### Slide 5 — Data Structure Design (2 minutes)

**What to say:**

> "Let me explain how data flows through the system from file to screen."

| Step | Data Structure | Why |
|------|---------------|-----|
| **CSV File** | Raw text (19MB, 114K lines) | Persistent storage |
| **Loading** | `BufferedReader` + `ArrayList<String>` (parsed fields) | Stream-based, line-by-line parsing — no loading entire file into a String |
| **In Memory** | `List<TrackRecord>` (ArrayList of objects) | O(1) indexed access, cache-friendly iteration |
| **Genre Lookup** | `TreeSet<String>` → converted to `ArrayList<String>` | `TreeSet` auto-sorts genres alphabetically; converted to `ArrayList` for ListView display |
| **Filtering** | New `ArrayList<TrackRecord>` (subset) | Creates a new list — original is untouched |
| **Sorting** | In-place on the filtered `ArrayList` | Merge Sort uses an auxiliary `Object[]` array — sorts the existing list without copying |

> "We chose `ArrayList` over `LinkedList` because: we iterate sequentially for filtering, and Merge Sort accesses by index during the merge step. `ArrayList` gives O(1) random access — `LinkedList` would degrade Merge Sort to O(n²)."

---

### Slide 6 — The Sorting Algorithm: Merge Sort (3 minutes)

**What to say:**

> "We implemented Merge Sort **from scratch** — no `Collections.sort`, no `Arrays.sort`. Here's why, how, and how fast it is."

#### Why Merge Sort?

| Algorithm | Best | Average | Worst | Stable? |
|-----------|------|---------|-------|---------|
| Bubble Sort | O(n) | O(n²) | O(n²) | Yes |
| Quick Sort | O(n log n) | O(n log n) | **O(n²)** | No |
| **Merge Sort** | **O(n log n)** | **O(n log n)** | **O(n log n)** | **Yes** |

> "Merge Sort is the only common sort that guarantees O(n log n) in **all cases**. For a university project on sorting algorithms, it's the most defensible choice."

#### How It Works — Visual:

```
Step 1: DIVIDE — Keep splitting in half until single elements
[8, 3, 7, 1, 5, 2, 9, 4]
[8, 3, 7, 1]        [5, 2, 9, 4]
[8, 3]  [7, 1]      [5, 2]  [9, 4]
[8][3]  [7][1]      [5][2]  [9][4]   ← 8 single-element lists

Step 2: CONQUER — Merge pairs back together in sorted order
[3, 8]  [1, 7]      [2, 5]  [4, 9]
[1, 3, 7, 8]        [2, 4, 5, 9]
[1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 7, 8, 9]    ← fully sorted
```

#### Time Complexity Proof:

```
T(n) = 2·T(n/2) + O(n)     ← divide into 2 halves + merge costs O(n)

Level 0:           n         ← 1 problem of size n
Level 1:      n/2     n/2    ← 2 problems of size n/2
Level 2:   n/4  n/4  n/4 n/4 ← 4 problems of size n/4
  ...
Level k:  1 1 1 1 1 1 1 1    ← n problems of size 1

Height of tree = log₂(n)
Work per level = O(n)  (every element touched once)
Total = O(n) × O(log n) = O(n log n)
```

#### Performance on Our Data:

> "On our 114K dataset, sorting a filtered subset of ~1,000 tracks takes **under 10 milliseconds**. The full sort operation is displayed in the status bar of the UI."

---

### Slide 7 — Real-World Scenario: Why Merge Sort Matters (2 minutes)

**What to say:**

> "Let me give a real-world scenario where Merge Sort is the right choice over Quick Sort."

> "Imagine **Spotify's internal playlist ranking**. When a user applies filters — say, 'show me my top workout songs' — the system needs to sort thousands of tracks. If they use Quick Sort and hit the worst-case O(n²) scenario, a sort that normally takes 5ms could take **2 seconds**. In a real-time music app, that's a noticeable lag. Merge Sort's **guaranteed O(n log n)** means predictable, consistent performance — no matter what data you throw at it."

> "Merge Sort is also used in:
> - **Java's `Collections.sort()`** (it's actually TimSort, a Merge Sort variant)
> - **Python's `sorted()`** (also TimSort)
> - **External sorting** — when data is too large for RAM, Merge Sort can sort data stored on disk by merging sorted chunks
> - **Database engines** — SQL `ORDER BY` often uses merge-based sorting"

---

### Slide 8 — Live Demo (2-3 minutes)

> "Let me demonstrate the running application."

**Demo script:**

1. Launch the app: `build.bat run`
2. Say: "The app is loading 114,000 tracks from the CSV. You can see the loading screen."
3. Window appears. Say: "Here's our main interface — styled like a retro ASCII terminal with white and light-blue theming."
4. Ctrl+Click `pop` and `dance` genres. Say: "I'm selecting multiple genres using Ctrl+Click — this is our multi-genre OR logic."
5. Type `50` in Min Popularity. Say: "Setting minimum popularity to 50 — this filters out obscure tracks."
6. Change Sort By to `ENERGY`. Say: "I'll sort by Energy — this means the highest-intensity tracks appear first."
7. Click **RECOMMEND**. Say: "And here are the Top 10 results. Notice the status bar shows how many tracks matched and the sort time."
8. Point out: "The ASCII table is formatted with Unicode box-drawing characters — all rendered in a JavaFX TextArea with a monospaced font."

>

## 🧠 Q&A Cheat Sheet — Anticipated Questions

### About OOP

**Q: Why use an interface AND an abstract class? Isn't that redundant?**

> "No — they serve different purposes. The **interface** (`AudioEntity`) defines the *contract* — it says what operations any audio entity must support, without any implementation. The **abstract class** (`BaseEntity`) provides *shared implementation* — the 13 private fields, getters, setters, and the `getCompositeScore()` formula. If I only used the interface, every class would duplicate the field declarations and getters. If I only used the abstract class, I couldn't later have a class that extends something else AND implements AudioEntity. The combination gives maximum flexibility."

**Q: Where is polymorphism actually used in the code?**

> "In `RecommendationEngine.formatTopN()`. The method accepts a `List<TrackRecord>`, but it calls `entity.toAsciiRow(rank)` through the `AudioEntity` interface reference. If we later add `PodcastRecord extends BaseEntity`, the same `formatTopN()` method works without any changes — that's polymorphism in action. The calling code doesn't need to know the concrete type."

**Q: Is encapsulation just making fields private?**

> "Making fields private is the mechanism, but the purpose is **controlled access**. By using getters and setters, we can add validation later — for example, in `setPopularity()`, we could reject values outside 0-100. If the field were public, any code could set it to -500 or 99999 with no guard. Getters/setters are the door — you can put a bouncer behind it."

---

### About Merge Sort

**Q: Why not use `Collections.sort()` which is already optimized?**

> "Two reasons. First, this is a **data structures and algorithms course** — the point is to demonstrate understanding of the algorithm, not call a library method. Second, `Collections.sort()` uses TimSort, which is a hybrid of Merge Sort and Insertion Sort. By implementing Merge Sort from scratch, we show we understand the divide-and-conquer principle, the recurrence relation, and the merge step — which is what the assignment is testing."

**Q: What happens if two tracks have the exact same score? Does the order matter?**

> "Merge Sort is a **stable sort** — meaning if two elements compare as equal, their original relative order is preserved. In our code, the `merge()` method uses `<=` when comparing, which prefers the left element on ties. So if Track A and Track B both have a Vibe Score of 72.4, and A appeared before B in the filtered list, A will still appear before B after sorting. This matters for reproducibility and predictable results."

**Q: What's the space complexity of your Merge Sort? Is O(n) memory a problem for 114K tracks?**

> "The space complexity is O(n) because we allocate an auxiliary `Object[]` array of the same size as the list being sorted. For 114K tracks, that's 114K object references — roughly 456KB on a 64-bit JVM (4 bytes per reference). The actual TrackRecord objects already exist in memory — the auxiliary array just holds references, not copies. So the memory overhead is negligible. Even if we sorted the entire 114K list, the extra memory would be under 1MB."

**Q: Could you implement Merge Sort iteratively instead of recursively?**

> "Yes. The iterative version uses a bottom-up approach: start by merging pairs of single elements, then pairs of 2-element sorted subarrays, then pairs of 4-element subarrays, doubling the width each iteration. It avoids recursion and the call stack overhead. The time complexity is still O(n log n), and space is still O(n). I chose the recursive version because it's more readable and directly mirrors the mathematical recurrence relation — which makes it easier to explain and defend."

---

### About Data Structures

**Q: Why ArrayList instead of LinkedList?**

> "Two reasons. First, Merge Sort accesses elements by index during the merge step (`list.get(i)`). On an ArrayList, `get(i)` is O(1). On a LinkedList, `get(i)` is O(n) because it walks the chain from the head. Using LinkedList would turn Merge Sort's merge step from O(n) to O(n²), destroying the algorithm's advantage. Second, ArrayList has better cache locality — the backing array is contiguous in memory, so CPU prefetching works efficiently."

**Q: How much memory does 114K TrackRecord objects use?**

> "Each TrackRecord has: 3 String references + 1 int + 1 long + 1 boolean + 3 doubles ≈ 13 fields. On a 64-bit JVM with compressed oops, that's roughly 13 × 4 = 52 bytes per object + 16 bytes header + String object overhead. Conservatively, ~150-200 bytes per track. For 114K tracks: 114,000 × 200 = ~23MB. Plus the ArrayList backing array (~456KB) and the JVM overhead. Total: well under 100MB — trivial for modern machines."

**Q: What if the CSV file had 10 million rows instead of 114K? Would your approach still work?**

> "Loading 10 million objects into memory would use ~2GB of RAM, which is possible but wasteful. For that scale, I'd switch to: (1) streaming the CSV and building a **genre-indexed HashMap** — `Map<String, List<Integer>>` where the integers are file offsets, (2) loading only matching genres into memory on demand, or (3) using a database with indexed queries. The current in-memory ArrayList approach is appropriate for 114K but doesn't scale to millions."

---

### About AI Utilization

**Q: Did the AI write this code, or did you?**

> "The AI generated the code, but **we architected every decision**. Before any code was written, we: analyzed the dataset structure, identified missing columns, asked 12 specific design questions, and approved a file-by-file architecture plan. The AI is the typist; we are the architects. We can explain every class, every method, every algorithm — as demonstrated by this presentation."

**Q: Why did the AI choose this specific OOP structure? Could it have been simpler?**

> "The AI chose interface + abstract class + concrete class because it demonstrates all four OOP principles in the most pedagogically clear way. A simpler approach would be a single `Track` class with no inheritance — but that wouldn't demonstrate Abstraction (no interface), Inheritance (no parent class), or Polymorphism (no override). The three-layer hierarchy is the minimum structure needed to showcase all four principles, which is exactly what a university project requires."

**Q: What would you change if you did this project again?**

> "Three things: (1) Add **unit tests** — we'd write JUnit tests for Merge Sort correctness, filter logic, and Vibe Score calculation. (2) Add **lazy loading** — instead of loading all 114K tracks at startup, build a genre index and load on demand for faster startup. (3) Add **more sort stability documentation** — we'd include a unit test proving that equal elements preserve their original order."

---

### About the Scoring Formula

**Q: Why equal weights? Isn't popularity more important than valence?**

> "Equal weights were a design choice for simplicity and fairness. Popularity measures how many people listen to a track — it's a social signal. Valence measures musical positiveness — it's an audio feature. They're fundamentally different dimensions. Giving them equal weight means the Vibe Score is a true average of all four factors. If we weighted popularity at 40%, the Vibe Score would be nearly identical to popularity alone, making the other three metrics irrelevant. Equal weights ensure all four features contribute meaningfully."

**Q: How would you validate that the Vibe Score actually recommends 'good' music?**

> "I'd run an **A/B test**: show two groups of users recommendations — one sorted by raw popularity, one sorted by our Vibe Score — and measure which group skips fewer tracks and listens longer. If Vibe Score works, users should stay engaged longer because the recommendations match not just what's popular, but what has the right energy, danceability, and mood for their context."

---

4. **If you get stuck on a question:** "That's a great question. Based on our implementation, the answer is..." (buy yourself 3 seconds to think).
5. **Divide among group members clearly.** Each person should own 1-2 sections and be able to answer follow-ups on their part.
6. **Transitions:** "Now that [Name] has explained the architecture, I'll walk you through how the sorting algorithm works."

## User

wait now explain to me the flow of the logic/backend and frontend of the program....smth a normal person can understand


## Assistant

The user wants a simple, non-technical explanation of how their program works — both the frontend (UI) and backend (logic). They want it explained in a way a "normal person" can understand. No jargon, no code. Just the flow.


## Assistant

# How The Program Works — Plain English

## Imagine a Music Store

Think of the program like walking into a **giant music store** with 114,000 CDs on the wall.

---

## Step 1 — Startup: Stocking the Shelves

When you launch the app, the first thing it does is:

> **Open the CSV file → Read every single row → Create a "card" for each song → Put all 114,000 cards in a big box in memory.**

That CSV file is just a spreadsheet. Each row is one song. The program reads it line by line, pulls out the song name, artist, genre, popularity number, etc., and turns each row into a neat little object it can work with.

**Takes about 2–5 seconds.** That's the loading screen you see.

---

## Step 2 — The Frontend: You Walk Into the Store

The window that pops up is the **storefront**. Here's what you see:

```
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│        SPOTIFY RECOMMENDATION SYSTEM        │
├─────────────────────────────────────────────┤
│                                             │
│  GENRES:                                    │
│  ☐ acoustic    ☐ pop     ☐ rock    ☐ jazz  │
│  ☐ hip-hop     ☐ metal   ☐ country ☐ ...   │
│  (you scroll through 114 genres)            │
│                                             │
│  Min Popularity: [____]                     │
│  Sort By: [VIBE_SCORE ▼]                    │
│                                             │
│  [► RECOMMEND]   [✕ CLEAR]                  │
│                                             │
│  ┌───────────────────────────────────────┐  │
│  │ RESULTS AREA (empty right now)        │  │
│  │                                       │  │
│  │                                       │  │
│  └───────────────────────────────────────┘  │
│                                             │
│  Status: "Ready. Select genres..."          │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

This is just a **form**. You fill it out, click the button, and the backend does the work.

---

## Step 3 — You Fill Out the Form

Let's say you do this:

1. You **Ctrl+Click** three genres: `pop`, `dance`, `electronic`
2. You type `50` in the Min Popularity box
3. You leave Sort By as `VIBE_SCORE`
4. You click **RECOMMEND**

That's you telling the store:

> "Show me songs that are pop OR dance OR electronic, and they have to be at least moderately popular (50+). Then rank them by their overall vibe."

---

## Step 4 — The Backend Goes to Work

This is where the magic happens. Three things occur, one after another:

### Thing A: Filter by Genre (the bouncer)

The program grabs the big box of 114,000 song cards and goes through them **one by one**:

```
Card 1: "Comedy" by Gen Hoshino — genre = "acoustic"  → ❌ not in your list, toss it aside
Card 2: "Blinding Lights" — genre = "pop"              → ✅ in your list, keep it
Card 3: "Levitating" — genre = "dance"                 → ✅ in your list, keep it
Card 4: "Bohemian Rhapsody" — genre = "rock"           → ❌ not in your list, toss it
...
(repeat 114,000 times)
```

After this step, you might have **~5,000 songs** left. The other 109,000 are gone.

### Thing B: Filter by Popularity (the second bouncer)

Now it takes those 5,000 songs and does another pass:

```
"Song X" — popularity = 82  → ✅ 82 ≥ 50, keep it
"Song Y" — popularity = 12  → ❌ 12 < 50, toss it
"Song Z" — popularity = 65  → ✅ 65 ≥ 50, keep it
...
```

Now you're down to maybe **~3,000 songs**.

### Thing C: Sort by Vibe Score (the ranking)

Now comes the sorting. Each of those 3,000 songs gets a **Vibe Score** calculated like this:

```
Vibe Score = (popularity + danceability×100 + energy×100 + valence×100) ÷ 4

Example for "Levitating" by Dua Lipa:
  popularity    = 88
  danceability  = 0.70  → ×100 = 70
  energy        = 0.82  → ×100 = 82
  valence       = 0.91  → ×100 = 91

  Vibe Score = (88 + 70 + 82 + 91) ÷ 4 = 82.75
```

Now the program **sorts all 3,000 songs from highest Vibe Score to lowest** using **Merge Sort**.

Here's how Merge Sort works in plain English:

> Imagine you have a messy pile of 3,000 cards with numbers on them.
>
> **Step 1:** Split the pile in half → two piles of 1,500.
> **Step 2:** Split each half again → four piles of 750.
> **Step 3:** Keep splitting until every pile is just **one card**.
>
> Now you have 3,000 piles of 1 card each. Every pile of 1 is "sorted" (there's only one card, so it can't be out of order).
>
> **Step 4:** Start merging. Take two piles of 1. Compare the numbers. Put the higher one first. You now have a pile of 2, sorted.
> **Step 5:** Take two piles of 2. Compare the top cards, pull the higher one, repeat. You now have a pile of 4, sorted.
> **Step 6:** Keep doubling — merge two piles of 4 into 8, then 16, then 32... until you have **one pile of 3,000, fully sorted**.
>
> This takes **3,000 × 12 steps ≈ 36,000 operations**. If you used a dumb sort (compare every card to every other card), it'd be **3,000 × 3,000 = 9,000,000 operations**. Merge Sort is **250× faster**.

This whole sort takes **under 10 milliseconds** on your computer.

---

## Step 5 — The Results Come Back

The backend now has a sorted list of 3,000 songs, highest Vibe Score at the top. It grabs the **top 10** and formats them into a text table:

```
┌──────┬──────────────────────────┬────────────────────┬────────────────┬─────────┐
│ Rank │ Track                    │ Artist             │ Genre          │  Score  │
├──────┼──────────────────────────┼────────────────────┼────────────────┼─────────┤
│    1 │ Levitating               │ Dua Lipa           │ pop            │   82.8  │
│    2 │ Don't Start Now          │ Dua Lipa           │ pop            │   80.1  │
│    3 │ Blinding Lights          │ The Weeknd         │ pop            │   79.5  │
│   ...│                          │                    │                │         │
│   10 │ ...                      │                    │                │         │
└──────┴──────────────────────────┴────────────────────┴────────────────┴─────────┘
```

It sends this text block back to the frontend.

---

## Step 6 — The Frontend Displays It

The JavaFX window takes that text block and **pastes it into the results TextArea**. You see it on screen. Done.

The status bar at the bottom updates:

> "✓ Found 3,000 tracks | Sorted by VIBE_SCORE | pop, dance, electronic"

---

## The Entire Flow in One Picture

```
You open the app
       │
       ▼
CSV file (114K rows) → parsed into 114K TrackRecord objects
       │
       ▼
You select genres + popularity + sort field → click RECOMMEND
       │
       ▼
BACKEND:
  1. Filter 114K → ~5K by genre (OR logic)
  2. Filter 5K → ~3K by popularity (≥ 50)
  3. Calculate Vibe Score for each of the 3K
  4. Sort 3K by Merge Sort (highest first)
  5. Take top 10 → format as ASCII table
       │
       ▼
FRONTEND:
  Display the table in the TextArea
       │
       ▼
You see results. Done.
```

---

## What Each File Does (One-Liner Version)

| File | Role |
|------|------|
| `Main.java` | The ignition key — starts the app, shows loading screen, launches the window |
| `AudioEntity.java` | A contract — "anything that is a song must have these properties" |
| `BaseEntity.java` | The blueprint — stores all the data fields (name, artist, genre, numbers) |
| `TrackRecord.java` | The actual song — fills in the blueprint and knows how to format itself as a table row |
| `TrackRepository.java` | The reader — opens the CSV, parses each line, handles errors, returns a list of songs |
| `MergeSorter.java` | The sorter — takes a list and sorts it using the divide-and-merge strategy |
| `RecommendationEngine.java` | The brain — filters songs, scores them, sorts them, formats the top 10 |
| `TerminalView.java` | The storefront — builds the window, the buttons, the text boxes, and shows results |
| `terminal.css` | The paint job — makes everything look like a white/light-blue retro terminal |

---

That's it. The frontend is a form you fill out. The backend filters, scores, sorts, and returns the top 10. The whole thing takes less than a second after you click the button.


## User

now explain it again but moe techinical, like when clicking some genre where does it storem in an array? like explain it in OOP way


## Assistant

The user wants a technical explanation of the program flow from an OOP perspective. They want to know things like:

- How do objects interact with each other?
- What happens under the hood in terms of data structures, method calls, object lifecycles?

Let me trace the exact execution path from UI interaction to result display, explaining every class, every data structure, every method call in the chain.


## Assistant

# Technical OOP Flow — Object by Object

## Phase 1 — Application Startup

### What happens when `build.bat run` executes

```
JVM starts
  │
  ▼
Main.main(args)
  │
  └─ calls launch(args)          ← JavaFX entry point (inherited from Application)
       │
       ▼
JavaFX runtime calls Main.start(primaryStage)
```

Inside `Main.start()`:

```java
// 1. Create loading screen UI
VBox loadingBox = new VBox(15);
Label loadingLabel = new Label("Loading Spotify Dataset...");
ProgressBar progressBar = new ProgressBar();

// 2. Show it immediately
primaryStage.setScene(new Scene(loadingBox, 450, 200));
primaryStage.show();

// 3. Spawn a BACKGROUND thread to load data
new Thread(() -> {
    List<TrackRecord> allTracks = loadDataset();  // ← this blocks the thread, NOT the UI
    // When done, switch back to JavaFX Application Thread
    Platform.runLater(() -> {
        launchMainApp(primaryStage, allTracks);   // ← builds the real UI
    });
}).start();
```

### Inside `loadDataset()` — The Repository Instantiation

```java
// Step A: Create the repository object
TrackRepository repository = new TrackRepository();
// This is just object construction — no heavy work yet.

// Step B: Call loadTracks(Path)
List<TrackRecord> allTracks = repository.loadTracks(Paths.get("dataset/dataset.csv"));
```

Inside `TrackRepository.loadTracks()`:

```java
public List<TrackRecord> loadTracks(Path csvPath) throws IOException {
    List<TrackRecord> tracks = new ArrayList<>();   // ← EMPTY ArrayList, capacity = 10 (default)

    BufferedReader reader = Files.newBufferedReader(csvPath);
    // BufferedReader wraps a FileInputStream — reads the file as a stream of chars.

    reader.readLine();  // skip the header line

    String line;
    while ((line = reader.readLine()) != null) {
        TrackRecord track = parseLine(line, lineNumber);  // ← returns a new TrackRecord or null
        if (track != null) {
            tracks.add(track);  // ← ArrayList dynamically resizes: 10 → 15 → 22 → 33 → ...
        }
    }

    return tracks;  // ← returns a fully-populated ArrayList<TrackRecord>
}
```

### What `parseLine()` does — Object Construction Chain

```java
private TrackRecord parseLine(String line, int lineNumber) {
    // 1. Parse the raw CSV string into fields
    List<String> fields = parseCsvLine(line);
    // Returns: ["0", "5SuOik...", "Gen Hoshino", "Comedy", "Comedy", "73", ...]

    // 2. Extract typed values
    String trackId   = fields.get(1).trim();     // "5SuOik..."
    String trackName = fields.get(4).trim();     // "Comedy"
    String genre     = fields.get(20).trim();    // "acoustic"
    int popularity   = Integer.parseInt(fields.get(5).trim());  // 73
    double dance     = Double.parseDouble(fields.get(8).trim()); // 0.676
    double energy    = Double.parseDouble(fields.get(9).trim()); // 0.461
    double valence   = Double.parseDouble(fields.get(17).trim()); // 0.715

    // 3. CONSTRUCT the TrackRecord object via inheritance
    return new TrackRecord(
        trackId, trackName, artists, albumName, genre,
        popularity, durationMs, explicit,
        danceability, energy, valence
    );
}
```

The constructor chain:

```
new TrackRecord(...)
    │
    └─ calls super(...)          ← BaseEntity's constructor
         │
         └─ assigns all 13 private fields:
              this.trackId = trackId;
              this.popularity = popularity;
              this.danceability = danceability;
              ...etc...

TrackRecord has no fields of its own.
It just inherits everything from BaseEntity.
```

**Result after startup:**
```
ArrayList<TrackRecord>   (in Main's memory)
  │
  ├─ [0] → TrackRecord @0x1a2b  (trackName="Comedy", popularity=73, ...)
  ├─ [1] → TrackRecord @0x3c4d  (trackName="Ghost - Acoustic", popularity=55, ...)
  ├─ [2] → TrackRecord @0x5e6f  (trackName="To Begin Again", popularity=57, ...)
  ...
  └─ [113999] → TrackRecord @0x...
```

One `ArrayList`. ~114,000 `TrackRecord` objects. Each object holds 13 fields.

---

## Phase 2 — UI Construction (MVC Wiring)

Back in `Main.launchMainApp()`:

```java
// 1. Create the CONTROLLER
RecommendationEngine engine = new RecommendationEngine();
// This object has no state. It's stateless — all methods take inputs and return outputs.
// No fields to store. Pure functions.

// 2. Create the VIEW, passing in the MODEL data and the CONTROLLER
TerminalView view = new TerminalView(stage, allTracks, engine);
// The view now holds references to:
//   - this.allTracks  → the ArrayList of 114K TrackRecords
//   - this.engine     → the RecommendationEngine
//   - this.primaryStage → the JavaFX window
```

Inside `TerminalView` constructor:

```java
public TerminalView(Stage primaryStage, List<TrackRecord> allTracks, RecommendationEngine engine) {
    this.primaryStage = primaryStage;
    this.allTracks = allTracks;     // ← STORES the reference (NOT a copy)
    this.engine = engine;           // ← STORES the reference
    buildScene();                   // ← builds all the JavaFX widgets
}
```

### What `


## User

now explain it again but moe techinical, like when clicking some genre where does it storem in an array? like explain it in OOP way. Not like that jsut give me the flow.... I can't understand code very well but I know words like interface or arraylist


## Assistant

The user wants a technical explanation of the program's flow, specifically in OOP terms, but at a level they can understand without reading code. They know terms like "interface" and "ArrayList" but can't read code fluently. They want to know things like "when I click a genre, where does it get stored?" - tracing the data through the objects.

Let me trace the exact data flow through the OOP objects:

1. User clicks genres in the ListView
2. Where are those selections stored?
3. What happens when RECOMMEND is clicked?
4. How do the objects interact?

I should explain it object-by-object, showing how data moves from one object to another, using terms like ArrayList, interface, object, method call, but without showing actual code.


## Assistant

# The OOP Data Flow — Object by Object

---

## Before You Even Click Anything — What Objects Exist in Memory

When the app finishes loading, these objects are sitting in memory:

**Object 1:** `TrackRepository` — already did its job. It's just sitting there. It read the CSV, created 114,000 `TrackRecord` objects, and now it's idle.

**Object 2:** An `ArrayList<TrackRecord>` containing 114,000 `TrackRecord` objects. This is the big box. Every song from the CSV is one `TrackRecord` object inside this ArrayList.

**Object 3:** `RecommendationEngine` — also idle. It hasn't been called yet. It's just waiting.

**Object 4:** `TerminalView` — this is the window on your screen. It holds references to Object 2 (the ArrayList) and Object 3 (the RecommendationEngine). It's the only object that talks to you.

**Object 5:** 114 `String` objects in the genre `ListView` — one for each genre name like `"pop"`, `"rock"`, `"acoustic"`.

---

## Step 1 — You Click Genres in the ListView

The genre list on screen is a JavaFX `ListView<String>`. It has a built-in object called a `SelectionModel`.

When you Ctrl+Click `pop`, then `dance`, then `electronic` — the `SelectionModel` stores those three strings internally.

**Where exactly?** Inside the `SelectionModel` object, there's an `ObservableList<String>` containing:

```
["pop", "dance", "electronic"]
```

That list lives inside the `ListView` object. It's not in an array you created — it's in JavaFX's own data structure.

---

## Step 2 — You Click RECOMMEND

The `RECOMMEND` button has an event handler inside `TerminalView`. When you click it, this method fires. Here's what happens, step by step:

### 2a — The genres are extracted

`TerminalView` asks the `ListView`'s `SelectionModel`: *"Give me everything the user selected."*

The `SelectionModel` returns an `ObservableList<String>` with three items. `TerminalView` copies those items into a **brand new `HashSet<String>`**:

```
selectedGenres = {"pop", "dance", "electronic"}
```

It's a `HashSet` (not an `ArrayList`) because `HashSet` lookups are O(1) — instant. The `RecommendationEngine` will use it to check "is this track's genre in the set?" millions of times, and `HashSet` is the fastest structure for that.

---

## Step 3 — The Popularity Field is Read

`TerminalView` looks at the `TextField` where you might have typed a number.

- If you typed `50` → it parses the string `"50"` into the integer `50`.
- If you left it empty → it uses `0` (meaning: don't filter by popularity).

This integer is stored in a local variable called `minPopularity`. It's not in any object yet — it's just a temporary value in the method.

---

## Step 4 — The Sort Field is Read

`TerminalView` looks at the `ComboBox` dropdown. The user selected `VIBE_SCORE` (or whatever). That value is an **enum constant** — one of five possible values defined inside `RecommendationEngine`:

```
SortField.VIBE_SCORE
SortField.POPULARITY
SortField.DANCEABILITY
SortField.ENERGY
SortField.VALENCE
```

This enum value is stored in a local variable called `sortField`.

---

## Step 5 — TerminalView Calls RecommendationEngine

Now `TerminalView` stops doing UI work and starts calling the controller. It makes this call:

```
engine.filterByGenres(allTracks, selectedGenres)
```

Let's break down exactly what happens inside this method call:

### Inside `filterByGenres`:

**Inputs received:**
- `allTracks` → the `ArrayList` of 114,000 `TrackRecord` objects
- `selectedGenres` → the `HashSet` with `{"pop", "dance", "electronic"}`

**What the method does:**

1. Creates a **new, empty `ArrayList<TrackRecord>`** called `filtered`.

2. Starts a `for` loop. It iterates through `allTracks` — one `TrackRecord` object at a time.

3. For each `TrackRecord`, it calls `.getGenre()` — that method returns a `String` like `"pop"` or `"acoustic"`.

4. It asks the `HashSet`: *"Does this string exist in you?"* — `selectedGenres.contains("pop")`.

5. If **yes** → the `TrackRecord` object reference is added to the `filtered` ArrayList.
   If **no** → the track is ignored.

6. After checking all 114,000 tracks, the `filtered` ArrayList contains roughly 5,000 `TrackRecord` objects.

**Important:** The `TrackRecord` objects in `filtered` are **the exact same objects** as in `allTracks`. No copies were made. Both ArrayLists hold references to the same objects in memory. It's like having two different index cards pointing to the same person's file.

7. The method returns the `filtered` ArrayList back to `TerminalView`.

---

## Step 6 — Popularity Filter

`TerminalView` now calls the second method:

```
engine.filterByPopularity(filtered, minPopularity)
```

**Inputs:**
- `filtered` → the ArrayList of ~5,000 tracks
- `minPopularity` → the integer `50`

**What happens inside:**

1. Creates another **new, empty `ArrayList<TrackRecord>`** called `filteredByPop`.

2. Loops through the 5,000 tracks.

3. For each track, calls `.getPopularity()` — returns an `int`.

4. Checks: `is this number >= 50?`

5. If **yes** → add to `filteredByPop`. If **no** → skip.

6. Returns the new ArrayList — now down to ~3,000 tracks.

Again: these 3,000 objects are the **same objects** from the original 114,000. Nothing was copied. We just filtered which references end up in which list.

---

## Step 7 — Sorting (Merge Sort)

`TerminalView` now calls:

```
engine.sortBy(filtered, sortField)
```

**Input:**
- `filtered` → the ArrayList of ~3,000 `TrackRecord` objects
- `sortField` → the enum value `SortField.VIBE_SCORE`

**What happens inside:**

1. The method looks at the `sortField`. It's `VIBE_SCORE`, so it creates a **`Comparator<TrackRecord>`** — an object whose entire job is to compare two `TrackRecord` objects and say which one is "bigger."

2. The `Comparator` works like this:
   - It receives two `TrackRecord` objects: let's call them `trackA` and `trackB`.
   - It calls `trackA.getCompositeScore()` → returns a `double`, let's say `82.75`.
   - It calls `trackB.getCompositeScore()` → returns a `double`, let's say `71.30`.
   - It compares them: `82.75` vs `71.30`. Since we want descending order, it says `trackA` comes first.
   - It returns a number: negative means `trackA` first, positive means `trackB` first, zero means they're equal.

3. `RecommendationEngine` then calls `MergeSorter.sort(filtered, comparator)`.

**What happens inside `MergeSorter.sort`:**

The `MergeSorter` object receives the ArrayList and the Comparator. It doesn't know what a `TrackRecord` is. It doesn't know what a Vibe Score is. It just knows: *"I have a list of things, and I have a way to compare any two of them."*

It then:
1. Creates an auxiliary `Object[]` array the same size as the ArrayList (~3,000 slots).
2. Calls its recursive `mergeSortRecursive(list, aux, 0, 2999, comparator)`.
3. The recursion splits the list, sorts each half, and merges them back together — using the `Comparator` to decide which element goes first at every comparison.
4. The ArrayList is **modified in-place**. The objects inside are rearranged — the highest Vibe Score ends up at index 0, the second highest at index 1, and so on.

The ArrayList now contains the same 3,000 `TrackRecord` objects, but in sorted order.

---

## Step 8 — Formatting the Top 10

`TerminalView` calls:

```
engine.formatTopN(filtered, 10)
```

**What happens inside:**

1. The method checks: *"How many tracks are in the list?"* → 3,000.
2. It calculates: *"Should I return 10 or 3,000? Whichever is smaller."* → 10.

3. It creates a `StringBuilder` — a mutable string object that you can append to without creating a new string each time.

4. It appends the ASCII table header — a `String` with box-drawing characters:
   ```
   ┌──────┬──────────────────────────┬...
   │ Rank │ Track                    │...
   ├──────┼──────────────────────────┼...
   ```

5. It starts a loop: `i` goes from 0 to 9.

6. For each iteration, it calls `filtered.get(i)` — this returns the `TrackRecord` at that index.

7. On that `TrackRecord`, it calls `.toAsciiRow(i + 1)`.

**This is polymorphism.** The method doesn't know it's calling `TrackRecord`'s version. It only knows the object is an `AudioEntity` (the interface). But at runtime, Java calls `TrackRecord.toAsciiRow()`, which returns a formatted string like:

```
│    1 │ Levitating               │ Dua Lipa           │ pop            │   82.8  │
```

8. That string is appended to the `StringBuilder`.

9. After all 10 rows, it appends the table footer (bottom border).

10. The method calls `.toString()` on the `StringBuilder` — this creates one giant `String` containing the entire ASCII table.

11. That `String` is returned to `TerminalView`.

---

## Step 9 — Display

`TerminalView` receives the giant `String`. It calls:

```
resultsArea.setText(tableString)
```

`resultsArea` is a JavaFX `TextArea` object. Its `setText()` method takes the string and renders it on screen in the monospaced font defined by the CSS.

The status bar `Label` object is also updated:

```
statusLabel.setText("✓ Found 3000 tracks | Sorted by VIBE_SCORE | pop, dance, electronic")
```

---

## The Complete Object Interaction Map

```
TerminalView (the window)
    │
    │ holds a reference to ─────────────────────────────────────┐
    │                                                           │
    ▼                                                           ▼
ListView<String>                                    RecommendationEngine
    │                                                           │
    │ SelectionModel stores:                                    │
    │   ["pop", "dance", "electronic"]                          │
    │                                                           │
    │ user clicks RECOMMEND                                     │
    │     │                                                     │
    │     ▼                                                     │
    │ copies to HashSet<String> ───────────────────────────────►│
    │   {"pop", "dance", "electronic"}              ┌───────────┘
    │                                               │
    │ passes ArrayList<TrackRecord> (114K items) ──►│
    │                                               ▼
    │                                    filterByGenres() returns:
    │                                    ArrayList<TrackRecord> (~5K items)
    │                                               │
    │                                    filterByPopularity() returns:
    │                                    ArrayList<TrackRecord> (~3K items)
    │                                               │
    │                                    sortBy() rearranges in-place
    │                                    using MergeSorter + Comparator
    │                                               │
    │                                    formatTopN() returns:
    │                                    String (ASCII table)
    │                                               │
    │ receives String ◄─────────────────────────────┘
    │
    ▼
TextArea.setText(tableString)
    │
    ▼
User sees results on screen
```

---

## Key Takeaway — What Gets Stored Where

| Data | Stored In | Type |
|------|-----------|------|
| 114K tracks at startup | `ArrayList<TrackRecord>` in `TerminalView` | ArrayList of objects |
| Selected genres | `SelectionModel` inside `ListView`, then copied to `HashSet<String>` | JavaFX internal list → HashSet |
| Popularity threshold | Local variable `minPopularity` | `int` |
| Sort field | Local variable `sortField` | Enum `SortField` |
| Filtered tracks (after genre) | New `ArrayList<TrackRecord>` (same object references, not copies) | ArrayList |
| Filtered tracks (after popularity) | Another new `ArrayList<TrackRecord>` | ArrayList |
| Sorted order | The filtered ArrayList is rearranged in-place by `MergeSorter` | Same ArrayList, different order |
| Auxiliary array for sorting | `Object[]` inside `MergeSorter` (temporary, garbage collected after) | Array |
| Final ASCII table | `StringBuilder` → converted to `String` → displayed in `TextArea` | String |

Every `TrackRecord` object is created once at startup and lives in memory until the app closes. Filtering doesn't copy them — it just creates new ArrayLists that hold references to the same objects. Sorting doesn't copy them either — it rearranges which index each reference sits at inside the ArrayList.

Let me create a clear flowchart first, then explain each file in order of how data flows through them.

```
         │
         │  returns sorted ArrayList back to RecommendationEngine
```

# Now, File by File — In Flow Order

Let me walk through each file **in the order data actually touches it**. I'll tell you exactly what each file is, what it stores, and what it passes to the next file.

---

## File 1 — `TrackRepository.java`

**What it is:** The CSV reader. It's the only file that ever touches the dataset file on disk.

**When it runs:** Once, at app startup. Then it goes idle.

**What it does step by step:**
1. Opens `dataset/dataset.csv` using a `BufferedReader` (reads the file one line at a time, doesn't load the whole file into memory at once).
2. Reads the header line and skips it.
3. For every remaining line (~114,000 lines):
   - Splits the CSV line into individual fields (track ID, artist name, song name, popularity number, genre, etc.)
   - Checks if required fields are present (if a line is broken/empty, it skips that line silently)
   - Creates a **new `TrackRecord` object** with those fields
4. Adds each `TrackRecord` into a growing `ArrayList<TrackRecord>`.
5. After the last line, returns the full `ArrayList<TrackRecord>` (114,000 objects).

**What it produces:** One `ArrayList<TrackRecord>` containing 114,000 `TrackRecord` objects.

**Who receives it:** `Main.java` calls `TrackRepository.loadTracks()` and gets the ArrayList back.

---

## File 2 — `Main.java`

**What it is:** The ignition key. The app entry point.

**When it runs:** When you launch the app. It runs once, sets everything up, then steps back.

**What it does step by step:**
1. JavaFX calls `Main.start()`. This is where the app begins.
2. Shows a **loading screen** (a simple VBox with a progress bar) so you see something while data loads.
3. Spawns a **background thread** — this is important because loading 114K tracks takes a few seconds, and if it ran on the main UI thread, the window would freeze.
4. Inside that thread, it calls `TrackRepository.loadTracks()` — the file we just covered.
5. Receives the `ArrayList<TrackRecord>` (114K objects).
6. Creates the **two core objects**:
   - `new RecommendationEngine()` — the brain/controller
   - `new TerminalView(stage, allTracks, engine)` — the window/view
7. Dismisses the loading screen and shows the main window.
8. `Main.java` is now done. It doesn't do anything else for the rest of the app's life.

**What it produces:** A `TerminalView` object (the window) that holds references to both the `ArrayList<TrackRecord>` and the `RecommendationEngine`.

**Who receives it:** Nobody. `Main.java` is the connector — it passes things to other objects and then exits the scene.

---

## File 3 — `AudioEntity.java` (Interface)

**What it is:** A contract. It defines what every "audio thing" in this system must be able to do.

**When it runs:** It never "runs." It's an interface — it has no executable code. It's a blueprint that other classes must follow.

**What it declares (the contract):**
- `getTrackId()` → returns a String
- `getTrackName()` → returns a String
- `getArtists()` → returns a String
- `getGenre()` → returns a String
- `getPopularity()` → returns an int
- `getDanceability()` → returns a double
- `getEnergy()` → returns a double
- `getValence()` → returns a double
- `getCompositeScore()` → returns a double
- `toAsciiRow(int rank)` → returns a String (this is the polymorphic method)

**Who implements it:** `BaseEntity.java` declares `implements AudioEntity`.

**Why it exists:** So that `RecommendationEngine` and `TerminalView` can work with *any* audio entity through this interface, without needing to know the concrete class. If you later added `PodcastRecord`, the same code would still work.

---

## File 4 — `BaseEntity.java` (Abstract Class)

**What it is:** The data blueprint. It implements the `AudioEntity` interface and provides the actual storage.

**When it runs:** It never runs on its own. It's abstract — you can't create a `BaseEntity` directly. It only exists to be extended.

**What it stores (13 private fields):**

| Field | Type | Example Value |
|-------|------|---------------|
| `trackId` | String | `"5SuOikwiRyPMVoIQDJUgSV"` |
| `trackName` | String | `"Comedy"` |
| `artists` | String | `"Gen Hoshino"` |
| `albumName` | String | `"Comedy"` |
| `genre` | String | `"acoustic"` |
| `popularity` | int | `73` |
| `durationMs` | long | `230666` |
| `explicit` | boolean | `false` |
| `danceability` | double | `0.676` |
| `energy` | double | `0.461` |
| `valence` | double | `0.715` |

All fields are **private**. The only way to read or change them is through getters and setters. That's encapsulation.

**What it provides:**
- A constructor that takes all 13 values (subclasses call this via `super(...)`)
- Getters and setters for every field
- **`getCompositeScore()`** — the Vibe Score formula:
  ```
  (popularity + danceability×100 + energy×100 + valence×100) ÷ 4
  ```
- An **abstract method** `toAsciiRow(int rank)` — declared but not implemented. Subclasses MUST provide the implementation.

**Who extends it:** `TrackRecord.java`.

**Who receives it:** Nobody directly. `BaseEntity` is an abstract parent — it lives inside every `TrackRecord` object.

---

## File 5 — `TrackRecord.java`

**What it is:** The concrete song object. Every row from the CSV becomes one `TrackRecord`.

**When it runs:** 114,000 of these are created at startup by `TrackRepository`. They sit in the `ArrayList<TrackRecord>` for the life of the app.

**What it does:**
- Its constructor calls `super(...)` passing all fields to `BaseEntity`. That's it — `TrackRecord` adds zero new fields.
- It **overrides** the abstract method `toAsciiRow(int rank)`.

**What `toAsciiRow` does:**
It takes this `TrackRecord`'s data (name, artist, genre, score) and formats it into one line of the ASCII table:

```
│    1 │ Levitating               │ Dua Lipa           │ pop            │   82.8  │
```

It handles truncation — if the song name is longer than 24 characters, it cuts it off and adds "...".

**Who calls it:** `RecommendationEngine.formatTopN()` calls `track.toAsciiRow(rank)` for each of the top 10 tracks. This is **polymorphism** — the engine calls the method through the `AudioEntity` interface, and Java resolves it to `TrackRecord`'s implementation at runtime.

**Who receives it:** The `ArrayList<TrackRecord>` in `TerminalView` holds all 114,000 of these objects.

---

## File 6 — `TerminalView.java`

**What it is:** The window. The entire GUI. Every button, text box, list, and label lives here.

**When it runs:** From the moment `Main.java` creates it, until you close the window. It's alive the whole time.

**What objects it holds:**

| Object | Type | Purpose |
|--------|------|---------|
| `allTracks` | `ArrayList<TrackRecord>` | The 114K tracks — kept as a reference, never modified |
| `engine` | `RecommendationEngine` | The controller — called when user clicks RECOMMEND |
| `genreListView` | `ListView<String>` | The genre selection list |
| `popularityField` | `TextField` | Where you type the min popularity number |
| `sortComboBox` | `ComboBox<SortField>` | The dropdown for choosing sort field |
| `resultsArea` | `TextArea` | The read-only box that displays the ASCII table |
| `statusLabel` | `Label` | The status bar at the bottom |

**What it does when you click RECOMMEND:**
1. Asks `genreListView.getSelectionModel()` for selected items → gets an `ObservableList<String>`.
2. Copies those into a `HashSet<String>` called `selectedGenres`.
3. Reads `popularityField.getText()` → parses to `int minPopularity`.
4. Reads `sortComboBox.getValue()` → gets a `SortField` enum.
5. Calls `engine.filterByGenres(allTracks, selectedGenres)` → gets back a filtered ArrayList.
6. Calls `engine.filterByPopularity(filtered, minPopularity)` → gets back a smaller ArrayList.
7. Calls `engine.sortBy(filtered, sortField)` → the ArrayList is now sorted.
8. Calls `engine.formatTopN(filtered, 10)` → gets back a big String (the ASCII table).
9. Calls `resultsArea.setText(tableString)` → the user sees results.

**Who receives from it:** Nobody. `TerminalView` is the end of the chain — it displays results to the human.

---

## File 7 — `RecommendationEngine.java`

**What it is:** The brain. All business logic lives here — filtering, scoring, sorting, formatting.

**When it runs:** Only when `TerminalView` calls it (when you click RECOMMEND). Otherwise it's idle.

**What methods it has:**

### `filterByGenres(ArrayList<TrackRecord>, HashSet<String>)`
- Receives the full 114K list and the set of selected genres.
- Creates a **new empty `ArrayList<TrackRecord>`**.
- Loops through all 114K tracks. For each one, calls `.getGenre()` and checks if that string is in the `HashSet`.
- If yes → adds that track to the new ArrayList.
- Returns the new ArrayList (~5K items after filtering).

### `filterByPopularity(ArrayList<TrackRecord>, int)`
- Receives the genre-filtered list and the minimum popularity number.
- Creates a **new empty `ArrayList<TrackRecord>`**.
- Loops through the list. For each track, calls `.getPopularity()` and checks if it's >= the minimum.
- If yes → adds to the new ArrayList.
- Returns the new ArrayList (~3K items).

### `sortBy(ArrayList<TrackRecord>, SortField)`
- Receives the filtered list and the chosen sort field.
- Creates a **`Comparator<TrackRecord>`** based on the `SortField`:
  - `VIBE_SCORE` → comparator calls `a.getCompositeScore()` vs `b.getCompositeScore()`
  - `POPULARITY` → comparator calls `a.getPopularity()` vs `b.getPopularity()`
  - `DANCEABILITY` → comparator calls `a.getDanceability()` vs `b.getDanceability()`
  - etc.
- Calls `MergeSorter.sort(list, comparator)` → the ArrayList is rearranged in-place.

### `formatTopN(ArrayList<TrackRecord>, int)`
- Receives the sorted list and the number 10.
- Builds an ASCII table:
  - Appends the header (borders + column names)
  - Loops 10 times. Each iteration: calls `list.get(i).toAsciiRow(i+1)` → appends the result
  - Appends the footer (bottom border)
- Returns the complete table as one giant `String`.

### `getAllGenres(ArrayList<TrackRecord>)`
- Called once at startup by `TerminalView` to populate the genre list.
- Loops through all 114K tracks, collects every unique genre into a `TreeSet<String>` (auto-sorts alphabetically).
- Returns the `TreeSet` as an `ArrayList<String>` (~114 genre names).

**Who receives from it:** `TerminalView` — every method returns data back to the View.

---

## File 8 — `MergeSorter.java`

**What it is:** A generic sorting machine. It knows nothing about music, tracks, or scores. It only knows how to divide a list and merge it back together.

**When it runs:** Only when `RecommendationEngine.sortBy()` calls it.

**What it receives:**
- An `ArrayList<TrackRecord>` (the filtered, unsorted list)
- A `Comparator<TrackRecord>` (the comparison rule — "compare by Vibe Score descending")

**What it does:**
1. Creates an auxiliary `Object[]` array the same size as the list.
2. Calls its private recursive method: `mergeSortRecursive(list, aux, 0, lastIndex, comparator)`.
3. The recursion splits the range in half repeatedly until each range is a single element.
4. Then it merges pairs back together, using the `Comparator` to decide which element goes first.
5. The merge writes elements back into the original `ArrayList` in sorted order.
6. The auxiliary array is discarded (garbage collected).

**What it returns:** Nothing. It modifies the `ArrayList` in-place. The list comes in unsorted and leaves sorted.

**Who receives from it:** `RecommendationEngine.sortBy()` — which then continues to `formatTopN()`.

---

## File 9 — `terminal.css`

**What it is:** The paint job. A stylesheet that makes the window look like a retro ASCII terminal.

**When it runs:** When `Main.java` creates the `Scene` and attaches the CSS file to it. JavaFX applies the styles to every UI component automatically.

**What it styles:**

| CSS Class | What It Targets | Key Styles |
|-----------|----------------|------------|
| `.main-panel` | The root layout | White background |
| `.title-label` | The ASCII art banner | Monospace font, light-blue background, blue border |
| `.control-panel` | The filter controls area | Light-blue background, blue border |
| `.genre-list-view` | The genre selection list | Light-blue background, blue highlight on selection |
| `.filter-text-field` | The popularity input box | Light-blue background, monospace font |
| `.filter-combo-box` | The sort dropdown | Light-blue background, monospace font |
| `.btn-recommend` | The RECOMMEND button | Blue background, white text, hover glow effect |
| `.terminal-textarea` | The results display area | Light-blue background, dark-blue monospace text |
| `.status-bar` | The bottom status bar | Light-blue background, blue text |

**Who receives from it:** Nobody. It's a passive resource file consumed by JavaFX.

---

## Complete File Connection Map

```
TrackRepository  ────(returns ArrayList<TrackRecord>)───→  Main.java
                                                               │
                            AudioEntity (interface) ←───────   │
                                  ↑                            │
                             BaseEntity (abstract) ←───────    │
                                  ↑                            │
                           TrackRecord (concrete) ←────────    │
                                                               │
                                                               ▼
MergeSorter ◄──(Comparator)── RecommendationEngine ◄──(user input)── TerminalView
     │                              │                                  │
     │  rearranges ArrayList        │  returns filtered/sorted         │  displays
     │  in-place                    │  data as ASCII String            │  results
     ▼                              ▼                                  ▼
  (modifies in-place)     ────────→ TerminalView ◄─────────────────────┘
                                          │
                                          ▼
                                     terminal.css (styling, applied once at startup)
```

That is every file, in flow order, showing exactly what data enters, what happens inside, and what data leaves.
